I N T R O D U C T I O N
BS EN 62366:2008
Medical practice is increasinglyusing MEDIcALoevtcEs for observationand treatment of
eATIENTS. Use ERRoRscaused by inadequateMEDIcALDEVIcEusABtLtrYhave become an
increasingcause for concern. Many of the MEDtcALDEVIcESdevelopedwithout applying a
usABtltryENGTNEERING pRocESSare non-intuitive,difficultto learn and to use. As healthcare
evolves,less skilledusens includingpATtENTs themselvesare now using MEDlcAtoevtcrs and
MEDIcALDEVIcESare becomingmore complicated.ln simpler times, the usen of a rueotcRl
DEVtcEmight be able to cope with an ambiguous,difficult-to-useusERINTERFAce. The design
of a usablenarorcnlDEVIcEis a challengingendeavour,yet many organizationstreat it as if it
were just "common sense'. The design of the usen tNTERFAce to achieve adequate(safe)
usABtLtryrequiresa very differentskill set than that of the technicalimplementationof that
interface.
The usnBlLtryENGINEERTNG pRocEssis intendedto achieve reasonableusABlLlrY,which in
t u r n i s i n t e n d e dt o m i n i m i s eu s e E R R o R sa n d t o m i n i m i s eu s e - a s s o c i a t e d R l s K S .S o m e ,b u t n o t
all, forms of incorrectuse are amenable to control by the MANUFAcTURER. The usABlLlrY
EN6INEERING pRocessis relatedto the RrsKMANAGEMENT pRocESSas indicatedin FigureA.1.
This InternationalStandard describes a usABtLtrYENGTNEERTNG PRocESS,and provides
guidance on how to implement and execute the pRocEss to provide SAFETYin MEDICAL
DEVIcES. lt is intendedto be useful not only for MANUFAcTURERS of tueotcRLDEVICES, but also
for technical committees responsible for the preparation of particular MEDIcALDEVIcE
standards.

This annex provides a rationalefor this InternationalStandard.lts purpose is to promote

This InternationalStandardfocuses on the usABrLtrYof the usER INTERFAcE of a MEDIcAL
DEVIcE.UsRerLrrv,in general,includesattributessuch as usER satisfactionand EFFtctENcY

This lnternationalStandarduses the concept of usE ERRon.The term was chosen over the
more commonlyused terms of "user error"or "humanerror" becausenot all errorsassociated
with the use of a MEDIcAL DEVTcE are the resultof oversightor carelessnesson the part of the
usERof the MEDIcAL DEVrcE.Much more commonly,usE ERRoRSare the direct result of poor
usERTNTERFAce design.
Some usERtNTERFAce designscontributeto usE ERRonbecausethey employ non-intuitiveor

The scope of this InternationalStandardapplieswhen a MEDIcAL DEVtcEis used accordingto

(incorrect mental model)
A mental model is a usER's conceptualmodel of how the MEDICAL DEVICEworks and is
structured.lf the usER'smental model is based on the knowledgeof why a MEDICAL DEVICE
works in a certainway, it is a structuredmodel and allows the usERto solve problemsthat
m i g h ta r i s ed u r i n gt h e u s e o f t h e M E D I c A L D E V t c E .l d e a l l y ,a u s E R ' Sm e n t a lm o d e lc a n b e e a s i l y
createdthroughinteractionwith the MeotcALDEVtceor it can be acquiredthroughexplanation
from TRAINING or AccoMpANylNG DocuMENr.The best mentalmodelsare self-evidentand need
n o e x p l a n a t i o n .
The mental model of how a thermostatcontrolsa PATIENT warmingsystem could includetwo
p o s s i b i l i t i e s .
It is a simple on/off switch that opens and closes under the control of a thermocouple
deviceto maintaina desiredtemperature.
It is tap that controlsthe rate of flow of hot air such that highertemperaturesettingsallow
a largervolumeof air to flow, which allowsfasterwarmingof the PATIENT.
The correct mental model for a typical PATTENT warming system is that the thermostatis a
simple on/off switch and not a tap for controllingrate of airflow.The consequericeof having
the incorrectmental model is the inefficientuse of energy in achievinga desired pRrtErur
temperature.lf a usEn has the incorrectmental model of the thermostatbeing a tap they
inefficienttyset the thermostatfirst too high and then too low to try to rapidly achieve a
desiredsteadytemperature.See also the rationalefor Clause6.

(informative)
Categoriesof useRaction
For the purposesof this standard,USERactionsor inactionscan be broadlycategorizedinto
actionsthat are foreseeableand those that are not foreseeable.Clearly,those usERactions
or inactionsthat are not foreseeablecannotbe dealt with by this or any other standard.This
lnternationalStandarddescribesa pRocessthat deals with those usERactions or inactions
that can be foreseen.These foreseeableeventscan be furthersubdividedbetweenintended
a n d u n i n t e n d e du s E Ra c t i o n so r i n a c t i o n s( s e e F i g u r eB . 1 ) .
I n F i g u r e 8 . 1 , i n t e n d e du s E R a c t i o n s o r i n a c t i o n st h a t f a l l w i t h i n N o R M A Lu s E c a n b e a
responsethat is intendedby the MANUFAcTURER and expectedby the usrn, i.e. coRREcrusE.
Alternately,the intended action or inactioncould result in a mistake or could result from
conduct that is beyond any additionalmeans of ntsx coNTRoLby the MANUFAcTURER, i.e.,
ABNoRMAL usE. This does not necessarilymean that nSNoRMAL usE resultsis a poor outcome
for the pAlENr. Often the clinicaljudgementof the usen indicatesthat such use is in the best
interestof the eATIENT. For the purposesof this standardunintendedactionsor inactionsare
alwaysclassifiedas slips or lapses.
Slips, lapses, and mistakes are all considered forms of usE ERRoR.In the usABlLlrY
ENGTNEERTNG pRocEss,it is helpfulto differentiatebetweenthese categorieswhile determining
the root-causeof a particularusE ERRonto help ascertainthose errors that can be mitigated
b y d e s i g n .
Slips and lapsesare errorsthat resultfrom some failurein the executionand/orstoragestage
of an action sequence,regardlessof whetheror not the plan that guidedthem was adequate
to achieveits objective.Whereasslips are potentiallyobservableas externalizedactions-not-
a s - p l a n n e d( s l i p so f t h e t o n g u e ,s l i p s o f t h e p e n , s l i p s o f a c t i o n ) ,t h e t e r m l a p s e i s g e n e r a l l y
reserved for more covert error forms, largely involving failures of memory, that do not
necessarilymanifestthemselvesin actual behaviourand can only be apparentto the person
who experiencesthem.
Mistakes can be defined as deficienciesor failures in the judgmental and/or inferential
pRocESSrsinvolvedin the selectionof an objectivewhetheror not the actionsdirectedby this
decision-schemerun accordingto plan (adaptedfrom I30l).

c . 1 Background

collectedby severalregulatoryauthorities[13]. These examplesare abbreviateddescriptions
of the actual events and have been modifiedto highlightthe distinctionbetweenABNoRMAL
use and usE ERRon.The adverseevents were classifiedas indicatedfollowingevaluationof
the designand the accoMpANyrNG DocuMENrof the MEDIcAL DEVIcES involved.
It is recognizedthat differentiatingusE ERRoRfrom ABNoRMAL usE is not alwaysan easy task
and so often requires careful investigation,analysis, and documentation.A careful
investigationmight includetrendingand root cause analysisas techniquesto classifyevents.
The fist of possiblecausesof use ERRoRwas extractedfrom Table E.2 of ISO 14971:2007.

D.2 Overview of the usnBtLlrY ENGINEERING PRocESS
D . 2 . 1 G e ne r a l
Regardlessof whether one is designing a revolutionarynew product or making minor
modificationsto an existing product,the associateduseslltrY ENGINEERING PRocESSinvariably
includes several specific usABtltryENGINEERTNG elementsor steps. FigureD.1 depicts the
usABtltryENGTNEERTNG aspectsof MEDTcAL DEVtcEdesign as a cycle of steps. This does not
imply that every design effort is requiredto follow a rigid prescriptionof developmentactivities.
On the contrary,the specificusnetlrryENGINEERINc activitiesat each step in the cycle (and the
associatedtime, effort,and expenserequired)vary with each developmenteffort.

D.2.9 Case study of the usABrLrryENGTNEERTNG pRocEss- Public access defibrillators
D.2.9.1 Case study context
The following case study on public-access automated external defibrillators (AEDs)

D . z . g . 2 D e s i g n c o n c e p t d e v e l o p m e n ta n d u s e n r e s e a r c h
Even with increasinguse of manual cardiopulmonaryresuscitation,most people who suffer
life-threateningarrhythmiasoutsidethe hospitaldo not survive.Early defibrillationis thought
to be the key interventionto improve the survival rate. Therefore,the concept for public-
accessautomatedexternaldefibrillators(AEDs)developed.
UsRarlrryENcINEERING methods such as context-dependentobservation,prototyping,and
error analysiswere used in the desiln of AEDs. The first prototypeswere designedthrough
observingdefibrillatoruse in context at fire stations.Storageand access were identifiedas
key issues,and were addressedin the first iterationof designrequirements.

resultantdata quality. Researchprotocolsare usuallydesirableand are sometimesa legal
requirement.The protocoltypicaltystipulatesthe rationalefor the research,the natureof the
subject population,the methods to be employed, and how the resultant data are to be
analyzedand interpreted.

D.4.6.2 Hardware usERI{TERFAGE
When designinga hardwareusERtNTERFAcE, specificationsmight include:
- a control panel layout drawing that shows the appearanceand arrangementof MEDICAL
DEVTCE displaysand controls.Such drawings are usuallyaugmentedby a'written rationale
that covers topics such as functionalgrouping, protectionagainst accidentalactuationof
controls,and viewingangle considerations;
- an anthropometricanalysis diagram (a graphical analysis of the physical relationship
between the MEDTCAL DEVTCE and individualsof varying size that establishesthe design's
physicalsuitabilityfor the intendedusERpopulation);
- a description of expected usER interaction with the displays and controls (e.9. how
controls and displays change as a result of MEDICAL DEVTCE internal events and usER
actions).
D.4.6.3 Software usER|]{TERFAGE
When designinga softwareusERtNTERFACE, specificationsmight include:
- all screen and window layoutsincludinglabelling,fonts, use of colour,and graphics;
- the appearanceand behaviourof all on-screencontrols;
- all dialog flow, includingaudibleevents;
- all hard copy report designs;
- a description of expected usER interaction with the displays and controls (e.9. how
controls and displays change as a result of MEDTCAL DEVTCE internal events and usER
actions).
D.4.6.4 Other useful usABlLlrYEl{clilEER|G tools

- a conceptualmodel diagram thai illustratesthe usERTNTERFACE high-levelstructure (see
F i g u r eD . 2 ) ;
- a usER INTERFAcE map - an illustration(typically a flowchart) showing the relationships
among various screens;
- a screen template- a generic layoutfor the computerscreens;
- a story board - a set of softwarescreen printoutsthat can be cross-indexedto templates
and written specifications;
- a style guide - a set of written rules that ensure consistencyby governingthe graphical
compositionof screensand means of interaction.

D.5.17 Workload assessment
Usen performancecan be impairedby excessivelyhigh or low workloads.MrotcALDEVtcEuse
can affectworkloadand workloadcan impacthow usERsinteractwith the MEDIcAL orvtcr [27],
[37]. Workload assessmenthelps to evaluateor predict the worker's cognitivecapacityfor
additional tasks. Workload can be measured using psychological (e.g. subjective
assessments,perhapsobtainedwith questionnaires), procedural(e.g. effectson standardized
performancemetrics), or physiological(e.g. changes in heart rate) techniques.Workload
assessmentmethods generallyneed to be vALtDATEo and can be technicallycomplex and
difficultto analyze.

Coverageof EssentialRequirementsof EG Directives

refevantessentialrequirementsas givenin AnnexI of the EC Directives93/42|EECand 98/79/EC.

requirementsof the Directivesconcerned.

BSI - British Standards Institution
BSI is the independent national body responsible for preparing
British Standards. It presents the UK view on standards in Europe and at the

international level. It is incorporated by Royal Charter.
Revisions
British Standards are updated by amendment or revision. Users of
British Standards should make sure that they possess the latest amendments or
editions.
It is the constant aim of BSI to improve the quality of our products and services.
We would be grateful if anyone finding an inaccuracy or ambiguity while using
this British Standard would inform the Secretary of the technical committee
responsible, the identity of which can be found on the inside front cover.
Tel: +44 (0)20 8996 9000. pa1; +44 (0)20 8996 7400.
BSI offers members an individual updating service called PLUS which ensures
that subscribers automatically receive the latest editions of standards.

BSI implementation of those that have been published as British Standards,
unless otherwise requested.
